                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome of Chenopodium quinoa"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2017 542:7641"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-02-08"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Chenopodium quinoa (quinoa) is a highly nutritious grain identified as an important crop to improve world food security. Unfortunately, few resources are available to facilitate its genetic improvement. Here we report the assembly of a high-quality, chromosome-scale reference genome sequence for quinoa, which was produced using single-molecule real-time sequencing in combination with optical, chromosome-contact and genetic maps. We also report the sequencing of two diploids from the ancestral gene pools of quinoa, which enables the identification of sub-genomes in quinoa, and reduced-coverage genome sequences for 22 other samples of the allotetraploid goosefoot complex. The genome sequence facilitated the identification of the transcription factor likely to control the production of anti-nutritional triterpenoid saponins found in quinoa seeds, including a mutation that appears to cause alternative splicing and a premature stop codon in sweet quinoa strains. These genomic resources are an important first step towards the genetic improvement of quinoa. Constructing a reference genome for quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa) allows for genetic diversity during the evolution of sub-genomes in quinoa to be characterized and markers that may be used to develop sweet commercial varieties are identified. In this issue, Mark Tester and colleagues report a reference genome for quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa), a highly nutritious crop that can grow under a wide range of environmental conditions. Long-read sequencing combined with optical, chromosome-contact and genetic maps was used to generate the allotetraploid genome. The authors also sequenced the genomes of additional diploid and tetraploid Chenopodium species, characterizing genetic diversity and the evolution of sub-genomes in the crop. In the process, Tester and colleagues identified a transcription factor that regulates the biosynthesis of bitter-tasting saponins in quinoa, as well as markers that might be used to develop sweet commercial varieties."/>

Unfortunately, few resources are available to facilitate its genetic improvement. Here we report the assembly of a high-quality, chromosome-scale reference genome sequence for quinoa, which was produced using single-molecule real-time sequencing in combination with optical, chromosome-contact and genetic maps. We also report the sequencing of two diploids from the ancestral gene pools of quinoa, which enables the identification of sub-genomes in quinoa, and reduced-coverage genome sequences for 22 other samples of the allotetraploid goosefoot complex. These genomic resources are an important first step towards the genetic improvement of quinoa. Constructing a reference genome for quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa) allows for genetic diversity during the evolution of sub-genomes in quinoa to be characterized and markers that may be used to develop sweet commercial varieties are identified. In this issue, Mark Tester and colleagues report a reference genome for quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa), a highly nutritious crop that can grow under a wide range of environmental conditions. Long-read sequencing combined with optical, chromosome-contact and genetic maps was used to generate the allotetraploid genome. The authors also sequenced the genomes of additional diploid and tetraploid Chenopodium species, characterizing genetic diversity and the evolution of sub-genomes in the crop. In the process, Tester and colleagues identified a transcription factor that regulates the biosynthesis of bitter-tasting saponins in quinoa, as well as markers that might be used to develop sweet commercial varieties."/>

Maughan</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff7">7</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Brigham Young University, College of Life Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.253294.b, 0000 0004 1936 9115, Department of Plant and Wildlife Sciences, Brigham Young University, College of Life Sciences, Provo, 84602, Utah, USA" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Mark-Tester" data-author-popup="auth-Mark-Tester" data-corresp-id="c1">Mark Tester<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="King Abdullah University of Science and Technology (KAUST)" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.45672.32, 0000 0001 1926 5090, Biological and Environmental Sciences &amp; Engineering Division (BESE), King Abdullah University of Science and Technology (KAUST), Thuwal, 23955-6900, Saudi Arabia" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> (quinoa) is a highly nutritious grain identified as an important crop to improve world food security. Unfortunately, few resources are available to facilitate its genetic improvement. Here we report the assembly of a high-quality, chromosome-scale reference genome sequence for quinoa, which was produced using single-molecule real-time sequencing in combination with optical, chromosome-contact and genetic maps. We also report the sequencing of two diploids from the ancestral gene pools of quinoa, which enables the identification of sub-genomes in quinoa, and reduced-coverage genome sequences for 22 other samples of the allotetraploid goosefoot complex. The genome sequence facilitated the identification of the transcription factor likely to control the production of anti-nutritional triterpenoid saponins found in quinoa seeds, including a mutation that appears to cause alternative splicing and a premature stop codon in sweet quinoa strains. These genomic resources are an important first step towards the genetic improvement of quinoa.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Quinoa <i>(Chenopodium quinoa</i> Willd., 2<i>n</i> = 4<i>x</i> = 36) is a highly nutritious crop that is adapted to thrive in a wide range of agroecosystems. It was presumably first domesticated more than 7,000 years ago by pre-Columbian cultures and was known as the ‘mother grain’ of the Incan Empire<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Risi, C. &amp; Galwey, N. W. The Chenopodium grains of the Andes: Inca crops for modern agriculture. Adv. Appl. Biol . 10, 145–216 (1984)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d24281e985">1</a></sup>. Quinoa has adapted to the high plains of the Andean Altiplano (&gt;3,500 m above sea level), where it has developed tolerance to several abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Adolf, V. I., Shabala, S., Andersen, M. N., Razzaghi, F. &amp; Jacobsen, S.-E. Varietal differences of quinoa’s tolerance to saline conditions. Plant Soil 357, 117–129 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24281e989">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hariadi, Y., Marandon, K., Tian, Y., Jacobsen, S.-E. &amp; Shabala, S. Ionic and osmotic relations in quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.) plants grown at various salinity levels. J. Exp. Bot. 62, 185–193 (2011)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24281e992">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jacobsen, S.-E., Mujica, A. &amp; Jensen, C. R. The resistance of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.) to adverse abiotic factors. Food Rev. Int . 19, 99–109 (2003)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d24281e995">4</a></sup>. Quinoa has gained international attention because of the nutritional value of its seeds, which are gluten-free, have a low glycaemic index<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gordillo-Bastidas, E., Díaz-Rizzolo, D. A., Roura, E., Massanés, T. &amp; Gomis, R. Quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.), from nutritional value to potential health benefits: an integrative review. J. Nutr. Food Sci. 6, 497 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1000">5</a></sup>, and contain an excellent balance of essential amino acids, fibre, lipids, carbohydrates, vitamins, and minerals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Vega-Gálvez, A. et al. Nutrition facts and functional potential of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.), an ancient Andean grain: a review. J. Sci. Food Agric. 90, 2541–2547 (2010)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1004">6</a></sup>. Quinoa has the potential to provide a highly nutritious food source that can be grown on marginal lands not currently suitable for other major crops. This potential was recognized when the United Nations declared 2013 as the International Year of Quinoa, this being one of only three times a plant has received such a designation.</p><p>Despite its agronomic potential, quinoa is still an underutilized crop<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Massawe, F., Mayes, S. &amp; Cheng, A. Crop diversity: an unexploited treasure trove for food security. Trends Plant Sci . 21, 365–368 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1011">7</a></sup>, with relatively few active breeding programs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zurita-Silva, A., Fuentes, F., Zamora, P., Jacobsen, S.-E. &amp; Schwember, A. R. Breeding quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.): potential and perspectives. Mol. Breed . 34, 13–30 (2014)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1015">8</a></sup>. Breeding efforts to improve the crop for important agronomic traits are needed to expand quinoa production worldwide. To accelerate the improvement of quinoa, we present here the allotetraploid quinoa genome. We demonstrate the utility of the genome sequence by identifying a gene that probably regulates the presence of seed triterpenoid saponin content. Moreover, we sequenced the genomes of additional diploid and tetraploid <i>Chenopodium</i> species to characterize genetic diversity within the primary germplasm pool for quinoa and to understand sub-genome evolution in quinoa. Together, these resources provide the foundation for accelerating the genetic improvement of the crop, with the objective of enhancing global food security for a growing world population.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Sequencing, assembly and annotation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Sequencing, assembly and annotation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We sequenced and assembled the genome of the coastal Chilean quinoa accession PI 614886 (BioSample accession code SAMN04338310) using single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing technology from Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) and optical and chromosome-contact maps from BioNano Genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol . 30, 771–776 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1030">9</a></sup> and Dovetail Genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res . 26, 342–350 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1034">10</a></sup>. The assembly contains 3,486 scaffolds, with a scaffold N50 of 3.84 Mb and 90% of the assembled genome contained in 439 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The total assembly size of 1.39 gigabases (Gb) is similar to the reported size estimates of the quinoa genome (1.45–1.50 Gb (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Palomino, G., Hernández, L. T. &amp; Torres, E. C. Nuclear genome size and chromosome analysis in Chenopodium quinoa and C. berlandieri subsp. nuttalliae. Euphytica 164, 221–230 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1040">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kolano, B., Siwinska, D., Pando, L. G., Szymanowska-Pulka, J. &amp; Maluszynska, J. Genome size variation in Chenopodium quinoa (Chenopodiaceae). Plant Syst. Evol . 298, 251–255 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1043">12</a>)). To combine scaffolds into pseudomolecules, an existing linkage map from quinoa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Maughan, P. J. et al. Single nucleotide polymorphism identification, characterization, and linkage mapping in quinoa. Plant Genome 5, 114–125 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1048">13</a></sup> was integrated with two new linkage maps. The resulting map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>) of 6,403 unique markers spans a total length of 2,034 centimorgans (cM) and consists of 18 linkage groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 7</a>), corresponding to the haploid chromosome number of quinoa. Pseudomolecules (hereafter referred to as chromosomes, which are numbered according to a previously published single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Maughan, P. J. et al. Single nucleotide polymorphism identification, characterization, and linkage mapping in quinoa. Plant Genome 5, 114–125 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1058">13</a></sup>) were created by anchoring 565 scaffolds to the linkage map, representing 1.18 Gb (85%) of the total assembly length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 1, Supplementary Data 2</a>). This assembly represents a substantial improvement over the previously published quinoa draft genome sequence, which contained more than 24,000 scaffolds with 25% missing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Yasui, Y. et al. Draft genome sequence of an inbred line of Chenopodium quinoa, an allotetraploid crop with great environmental adaptability and outstanding nutritional properties. DNA Res . 23, 535–546 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1069">14</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics for quinoa, <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature21370/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Predicted protein-coding and microRNA genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 4</a>) were annotated using a combination of <i>ab initio</i> prediction and transcript evidence gathered from RNA sequenced from multiple tissues using both RNA-seq and PacBio isoform sequencing (Iso-Seq) approaches (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2a</a>). The annotation contains 44,776 gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2b</a>), which is in line with sequenced tetraploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol . 33, 524–530 (2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1772">15</a></sup>, and includes 33,365 genes with annotation edit distance (AED)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Eilbeck, K., Moore, B., Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. Quantitative measures for the management and comparison of annotated genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 67 (2009)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1776">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Yandell, M. &amp; Ence, D. A beginner’s guide to eukaryotic genome annotation. Nat. Rev. Genet . 13, 329–342 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1779">17</a></sup> values ≤ 0.3 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2c</a>). Of the genome, 64% was found to be repetitive, including a large proportion of long terminal repeat (LTR) transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 1</a>). A majority (97.3%) of the 956 genes in the Plantae BUSCO dataset<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1789">18</a></sup> were identified in the annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 3</a>), which is suggestive of a complete assembly and annotation. The utility of the assembly, linkage maps, and annotation was demonstrated by mapping the betalain locus and identifying candidate genes underlying stem pigmentation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Information 7.1.6</a>), which is often used as a morphological marker in breeding programs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Evolutionary history of quinoa"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Evolutionary history of quinoa</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Quinoa resulted from the hybridization of ancestral A- and B-genome diploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kolano, B. et al. Molecular and cytogenetic evidence for an allotetraploid origin of Chenopodium quinoa and C. berlandieri (Amaranthaceae). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol . 100, 109–123 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1808">19</a></sup>. Single-gene sequencing studies previously identified pools of North American and Eurasian diploids as candidate sources of the A and B sub-genomes, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Brown, D. C., Cepeda-Cornejo, V., Maughan, P. J. &amp; Jellen, E. N. Characterization of the granule-bound starch synthase I gene in Chenopodium. Plant Genome 8, 1 (2014)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1812">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Štorchová, H., Drabešová, J., Cháb, D., Kolár˘, J. &amp; Jellen, E. N. The introns in flowering locus T-like (FTL) genes are useful markers for tracking paternity in tetraploid Chenopodium quinoa Willd. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol . 62, 913–925 (2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1815">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Walsh, B. M., Adhikary, D., Maughan, P. J., Emshwiller, E. &amp; Jellen, E. N. Chenopodium polyploidy inferences from salt overly sensitive 1 (SOS1) data. Am. J. Bot . 102, 533–543 (2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1818">22</a></sup>, with hybridization occurring somewhere in North America. To understand genome structure and evolution in quinoa further, we sequenced, assembled, and annotated the A-genome diploid <i>C. pallidicaule</i> (commonly called cañahua or kañiwa) and the B-genome diploid <i>C. suecicum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Štorchová, H., Drabešová, J., Cháb, D., Kolár˘, J. &amp; Jellen, E. N. The introns in flowering locus T-like (FTL) genes are useful markers for tracking paternity in tetraploid Chenopodium quinoa Willd. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol . 62, 913–925 (2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1827">21</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Tab1">Table 1</a>). A high proportion of orthologous gene pairs in quinoa showed similar rates of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>), indicative of a whole-genome duplication event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). This probably represents the hybridization of ancestral diploid species, because a similar peak was not observed in <i>C. pallidicaule</i> or <i>C. suecicum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). Using mutation rates calculated for <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Koch, M. A., Haubold, B. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Comparative evolutionary analysis of chalcone synthase and alcohol dehydrogenase loci in Arabidopsis, Arabis, and related genera (Brassicaceae). Mol. Biol. Evol . 17, 1483–1498 (2000)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1857">23</a></sup> and for core eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151–1155 (2000)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1861">24</a></sup>, we estimate the tetraploidization to have occurred 3.3–6.3 million years ago.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Evolutionary history of quinoa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Evolutionary history of quinoa.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="589" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Seeds of <i>C. suecicum</i>, <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and quinoa. <b>b</b>, The proportion of gene pairs in each species binned according to <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values. <b>c</b>, Maximum likelihood tree generated from 3,132 SNPs. Black branches, diploid species. Coloured branches, tetraploid species: red, quinoa; blue, <i>C. berlandieri</i>; yellow, <i>C. hircinum</i>. Branch values represent the percentage of 1,000 bootstrap replicates that support the topology. Scale bar represents substitutions per site. <b>d</b>, Evolutionary relationships of <i>Chenopodium</i> species, showing the hypothesized long-range dispersal of an ancestral <i>C. berlandieri</i> to South America, and the eventual domestication of quinoa from <i>C. hircinum</i>, either from a single event that gave rise to highland and subsequently coastal quinoa (1), or in two events that gave rise to highland (2a) and coastal (2b) quinoa independently. Blue, red and yellow shading represents the geographic distribution of <i>C. berlandieri</i>, quinoa and <i>C. hircinum</i>, respectively.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM113">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM114">Source data</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Multiple interfertile tetraploid species have arisen from the ancestral tetraploid following hybridization, including <i>C. berlandieri</i> and <i>C. hircinum</i>, although the evolutionary relationships among quinoa and its diploid and tetraploid relatives remain unclear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wilson, H. D. Quinua biosystematics II: free-living populations. Econ. Bot . 42, 478–494 (1988)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d24281e1950">25</a></sup>. To begin to resolve these issues, we re-sequenced 15 additional quinoa samples representing the two major recognized groups of quinoa: highland and coastal (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 5</a>). We also sequenced five accessions of <i>C. berlandieri</i> and one accession each of <i>C. hircinum</i> from the Pacific and Atlantic Andean watersheds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 5</a>). Phylogenetic analysis of these taxa indicates that North American <i>C. berlandieri</i> is the basal member of the species complex (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). Quinoa was thought to have been domesticated from <i>C. hircinum</i> in a single event, from which coastal quinoa was later derived (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>, arrow 1); however, our sequencing data place a <i>C. hircinum</i> sample basal to coastal ecotypes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), suggesting the possibility that quinoa was domesticated independently in highland and coastal environments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>, arrows 2a and 2b, respectively). Future analyses with deeper sampling of quinoa and <i>C. hircinum</i> will help clarify the relationship between <i>C. hircinum</i> and quinoa, as well as provide germplasm for breeding broadly adapted coastal quinoa cultivars for warm-season production. The SNPs identified between these accessions and the reference quinoa genome—a total of 7,809,381 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 5</a>), including 2,668,694 that are specific to quinoa—will be useful in assessing genetic diversity and identifying genomic regions associated with desirable traits.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Analysis of sub-genome structure"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Analysis of sub-genome structure</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>By mapping sequencing reads from <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i> onto the quinoa scaffold assembly, and by performing BLASTN searches of each diploid against the quinoa assembly, 156 and 410 quinoa scaffolds (totalling 202.6 and 646.3 Mb) were assigned to the A and B sub-genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 6</a>). A mini-satellite repeat (18-24J) previously shown to be more abundant in the B sub-genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kolano, B. et al. Chromosomal localization of two novel repetitive sequences isolated from the Chenopodium quinoa Willd. genome. Genome 54, 710–717 (2011)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2022">26</a></sup> is over-represented in scaffolds assigned to the B sub-genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 6</a>). Nine chromosomes were assigned to each sub-genome (chromosomes hereafter designated as <i>Cq</i>A or <i>Cq</i>B, followed by the chromosome number), with the B sub-genome accounting for a larger percentage of both the genetic (1,087 cM) and physical (660 Mb) sizes than the A sub-genome (946 cM, 524 Mb). This result was not unexpected, given the differences in the estimated genome sizes of <i>C. suecicum</i> (815 Mb) and <i>C. pallidicaule</i> (452 Mb) based on <i>k</i>-mer analyses.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Identification and characterization of quinoa sub-genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Identification and characterization of quinoa sub-genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="640" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Blue lines and green lines connect regions of the <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i> genomes, respectively, with their orthologous regions in the quinoa genome based on BLASTN. Quinoa scaffolds are arranged based on their positions in linkage groups, with blue- and green-coloured bars indicating sub-genome assignment based on mapped reads from the two diploid species. Scaffolds that could not be unambiguously assigned to a sub-genome based on read mapping are shown in white. Grey bars separate neighbouring scaffolds. <b>b</b>, Homoeologous gene pairs in the A (blue chromosomes) and B (green chromosomes) sub-genomes. <b>c</b>, Simplified representation of synteny between <i>Cq</i>A12, <i>Cq</i>B05, <i>Cq</i>B03 and <i>Cq</i>A10. Dotted lines connect large-scale syntenic regions between the A (blue) and B (green) sub-genomes. The scale bar indicates approximate positions defining the indicated syntenic blocks. For purposes of visualization, <i>Cq</i>B05 and <i>Cq</i>A10 were inverted. <b>d</b>, Syntenic relationships between <i>B. vulgaris</i> and the A and B sub-genomes of quinoa. Colours distinguish quinoa regions syntenic to each <i>B. vulgaris</i> chromosome. Blue and green quinoa chromosomes indicate the A and B sub-genomes, respectively.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM115">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Visualization of the chromosomal locations of 5,807 homoeologous gene pairs revealed a high degree of synteny between the A and B sub-genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). A small number of homoeologous pairs (3.1%) mapped within the same sub-genome, suggesting that recombination and chromosomal rearrangements have occurred between the A and B sub-genomes. For example, we identified homoeologous A and B sub-genome regions located in the B sub-genome chromosomes <i>Cq</i>B05 and <i>Cq</i>B03. The genes in the region of ~70–72 Mb of <i>Cq</i>B03 are phylogenetically more similar to the A-genome diploid <i>C. suecicum</i> and therefore probably originated from the A sub-genome chromosome <i>Cq</i>A12 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). We also found evidence of large chromosomal rearrangements within sub-genomes, complicating the assignment of homoeologous chromosome pairs. For example, orthologue analysis clearly identifies <i>Cq</i>B05 and <i>Cq</i>A12 as homoeologous, although the same analysis is much more complicated with other chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). To clarify these relationships, we identified syntenic regions between chromosomes of the diploid <i>Beta vulgaris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Dohm, J. C. et al. The genome of the recently domesticated crop plant sugar beet (Beta vulgaris). Nature 505, 546–549 (2014)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2152">27</a></sup> (<i>n</i> = 9) and the A and B sub-genome chromosomes of quinoa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). These results indicate that <i>Cq</i>A02 and <i>Cq</i>A04 are orthologous to <i>B. vulgaris</i> chromosomes 8 and 2 (<i>Bv</i>chr8 and <i>Bv</i>chr2), respectively, whereas <i>Cq</i>B01 appears to be the result of a chromosome fusion. Likewise, <i>Cq</i>A07 appears to be the result of a fusion between ancestral chromosomes orthologous to <i>Bv</i>chr3 and <i>Bv</i>chr7.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Analysis of sub-genome content"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Analysis of sub-genome content</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>We used OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res . 13, 2178–2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2198">28</a></sup> to identify clusters of orthologous genes in related species in Amaranthaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 4</a>), and specifically investigated the retention and loss of orthologous genes in quinoa and the three diploid species <i>C. pallidicaule</i>, <i>C. suecicum</i> and <i>B. vulgaris</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Using sets of genes for which only one copy could be found in quinoa and in each of the diploid species, we found a similar number (1,031 and 849) of genes lost from the A and B sub-genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). The previously discussed set of 5,807 homoeologous gene pairs in quinoa are present as single copies in each of the diploids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Figs 2b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig3">3b</a>) and therefore represent a core set of single-copy genes retained in each genome and sub-genome. We also investigated genes retained in multiple copies. We found that quinoa, like <i>C. rubrum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cháb, D., Kolár, J., Olson, M. S. &amp; Storchová, H. Two flowering locus T (FT) homologs in Chenopodium rubrum differ in expression patterns. Planta 228, 929–940 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2233">29</a></sup> and <i>B. vulgaris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Pin, P. A. et al. An antagonistic pair of FT homologs mediates the control of flowering time in sugar beet. Science 330, 1397–1400 (2010)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2239">30</a></sup>, contains two genes that are orthologous to the <i>A. thaliana</i> gene <i>FT</i>, which regulates flowering time. Quinoa contains two homoeologous copies of <i>FT2</i> and three of <i>FT1</i>, owing to a local tandem duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 5</a>). <i>FT</i> is known to promote flowering in <i>A. thaliana</i>, and functional orthologues have been found in other species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Pin, P. A. &amp; Nilsson, O. The multifaceted roles of FLOWERING LOCUS T in plant development. Plant Cell Environ . 35, 1742–1755 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2268">31</a></sup>; however, <i>B. vulgaris</i> was found to contain a second <i>FT</i> gene that acts antagonistically by repressing flowering before vernalization. Future functional studies will help determine the function of these duplicated genes in quinoa, which, unlike <i>B. vulgaris</i>, does not require vernalization.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Sub-genome gene loss and retention."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Sub-genome gene loss and retention.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="190" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The number of orthologous protein-coding gene clusters shared between or unique to quinoa, <i>C. pallidicaule</i>, <i>C. suecicum</i> and <i>B. vulgaris</i>. <b>b</b>, The number of gene sets for which each gene has been retained as a single copy in each genome/sub-genome (middle), or lost from the quinoa A (left) or B (right) sub-genome. <b>c</b>, Maximum likelihood tree of flowering locus T (<i>FT</i>) sequences, indicating the presence of two sets of orthologues in quinoa and <i>B. vulgaris</i>. The tree is rooted on the branch containing <i>FT</i> from <i>A. thaliana</i>. Branch values represent the percentage of 1,000 bootstrap replicates that support the topology. Scale bar represents substitutions per site.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM116">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Mechanisms underlying saponin production"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Mechanisms underlying saponin production</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Quinoa seeds contain a mixture of triterpene glycosides called saponins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kuljanabhagavad, T., Thongphasuk, P., Chamulitrat, W. &amp; Wink, M. Triterpene saponins from Chenopodium quinoa Willd. Phytochemistry 69, 1919–1926 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2347">32</a></sup>. Although saponins may be beneficial for plant growth (for example, by deterring herbivory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="de Geyter, E., Lambert, E., Geelen, D. &amp; Smagghe, G. Novel advances with plant saponins as natural insecticides to control pest insects. Pers. Technol . 1, 96–105 (2007)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2351">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kuljanabhagavad, T. &amp; Wink, M. Biological activities and chemistry of saponins from Chenopodium quinoa Willd. Phytochem. Rev . 8, 473–490 (2009)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2354">34</a></sup>), they must be removed before human consumption as they are haemolytic and produce a bitter flavour. Because this process is costly, is often water-intensive, and can reduce the nutritional value of the seeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Konishi, Y., Hirano, S., Tsuboi, H. &amp; Wada, M. Distribution of minerals in quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.) seeds. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem . 68, 231–234 (2004)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2358">35</a></sup>, the development of saponin-free lines is a major breeding objective in quinoa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zurita-Silva, A., Fuentes, F., Zamora, P., Jacobsen, S.-E. &amp; Schwember, A. R. Breeding quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.): potential and perspectives. Mol. Breed . 34, 13–30 (2014)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2362">8</a></sup>. We found that saponins accumulate in the seed pericarp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 6</a>) between 20 and 24 days after anthesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), eventually accounting for 4% (w/w) of the mature seed mass (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Information 8.1</a>). We identified and annotated 43 different saponins in the seeds of the reference sample (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 9</a>), adding to the almost 100 different saponins that have been previously identified in different samples of quinoa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kuljanabhagavad, T., Thongphasuk, P., Chamulitrat, W. &amp; Wink, M. Triterpene saponins from Chenopodium quinoa Willd. Phytochemistry 69, 1919–1926 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2382">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Madl, T., Sterk, H., Mittelbach, M. &amp; Rechberger, G. N. Tandem mass spectrometric analysis of a complex triterpene saponin mixture of Chenopodium quinoa. J. Am. Soc. Mass Spectrom. 17, 795–806 (2006)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2385">36</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Candidate gene underlying saponin production."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Candidate gene underlying saponin production.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="442" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Imaging mass spectrometry visualization of selected masses, including saponins in the pericarp of a quinoa seed. Purple gradient bar, tentative phosphatidylcholine-(34:1), ([M + Na]<sup>+</sup> <i>m/z</i> = 782.5610, calc. 782.5670, 7.7 p.p.m. error); yellow gradient bar, tentative triacylglycerol-(54:6), ([M + K]<sup>+</sup> <i>m/z</i> = 917.6971, calc. 917.6995, 2.6 p.p.m. error); green gradient bar indicates a representative saponin phytolaccagenic acid with sugar chains hexose–pentose–hexose ([M + K]<sup>+</sup> <i>m/z</i> = 1173.5114, calc. 1173.509, − 2.0 p.p.m. error). Coloured bars represent the ion signal intensity scaled from 0% (bottom) to 50% (top) of maximum signal. Scale bars, 500 μm. <b>b</b>, Accumulation of saponins as measured by total acids during seed development. Illustrations represent fruit development at 12 and 24 days after anthesis. <i>n</i> = 6, 5, 5 and 5, respectively, for 12, 16, 20 and 24 days after anthesis. <b>c</b>, The percentage difference in allele frequency of sweet progeny compared to bitter progeny in the Kurmi × 0654 (top) and Atlas × Carina Red (bottom) populations. Alternating red and blue dots indicate positions of markers along alternating chromosomes, with unmapped markers in chromosome 0 shown in grey. Asterisk above the top panel indicates the approximate position of <i>TSARL1</i>. <b>d</b>, The saponin biosynthetic pathway, showing enzymes that catalyse each step of the pathway and the quinoa gene ID for genes encoding each enzyme. Boxes surrounding each gene ID are coloured according to their fold change in expression (log<sub>2</sub>) in sweet lines compared to bitter lines of Kurmi × 0654. Horizontal lines to the left of each gene ID represent the 2-kb region upstream of the start codon of each gene, with tick marks indicating the positions of motifs putatively recognized by TSARL1. <b>e</b>, Gene models of <i>TSARL1</i> in bitter and sweet lines. Red asterisk, premature stop codon in sweet lines of Kurmi × 0654.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM117">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM118">Source data</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Naturally occurring sweet quinoa strains that contain very low levels of saponins are present within the quinoa germplasm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Mastebroek, D. H., Limburg, H., Gilles, T. &amp; Marvin, H. J. P. Occurrence of sapogenins in leaves and seeds of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd). J. Sci. Food Agric. 80, 152–156 (2000)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2466">37</a></sup>, although the underlying genes regulating the absence of saponins in these lines are unknown. To identify these genes, we performed linkage mapping and bulk segregant analysis (BSA) using two populations segregating for the presence of saponins in the seeds: Kurmi (sweet) × 0654 (bitter), and Atlas (sweet) × Carina Red (bitter). Consistent with reports from other populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ward, S. M. A recessive allele inhibiting saponin synthesis in two lines of Bolivian quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.). J. Hered . 92, 83–86 (2001)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2470">38</a></sup>, segregation ratios in these populations indicate that the presence of seed saponins is controlled by a single gene, with the presence of saponins being dominant (71 bitter and 21 sweet in Kurmi × 0654; 567 bitter and 175 sweet in Atlas × Carina Red). We note that quantitative and qualitative differences exist in the saponins identified in bitter lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Table 8</a>) and that the presence and absence of saponins was correlated with differences in seed coat thickness, with bitter lines having significantly thicker seed coats than sweet lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig12">Extended Data Fig. 8</a>).</p><p>Linkage mapping and BSA in each population identified the same region on <i>Cq</i>B16 that distinguishes the bitter and sweet lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). Frequencies of the sweet allele in both populations reached 100% for markers located in <i>Cq</i>B16 on scaffold 3489. We investigated the genes in a 700-kb window surrounding this region of 100% sweet allele frequency. Of the 54 annotated genes in this region (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 7</a>), two are similar to genes previously shown to play a role in saponin biosynthesis. Specifically, AUR62017204 and AUR62017206 are neighbouring genes annotated as basic helix–loop–helix (bHLH) transcription factors sharing homology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 9a</a>) with the class IVa bHLH genes that are known to regulate triterpenoid biosynthesis in <i>Medicago trancatula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Mertens, J. et al. The bHLH transcription factors TSAR1 and TSAR2 regulate triterpene saponin biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant Physiol . 170, 194–210 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2504">39</a></sup>. In <i>M. truncatula</i>, overexpression of the <i>triterpene saponin biosynthesis activating regulator 1</i> (<i>TSAR1</i>) and <i>TSAR2</i> bHLH transcription factors was recently shown to increase the expression of genes in the triterpenoid biosynthetic pathway, resulting in increased accumulation of triterpene saponins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Mertens, J. et al. The bHLH transcription factors TSAR1 and TSAR2 regulate triterpene saponin biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant Physiol . 170, 194–210 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2521">39</a></sup>. TSAR1 and TSAR2 were also found to bind to the DNA motif 5′-CACGHG-3′ (where H can be A, C, or T)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Mertens, J. et al. The bHLH transcription factors TSAR1 and TSAR2 regulate triterpene saponin biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant Physiol . 170, 194–210 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2525">39</a></sup>. In quinoa, we found that AUR62017206 (hereafter <i>TSAR-like 2</i>, <i>TSARL2</i>) was expressed in root tissue but not in flowers or immature seeds, whereas AUR62017204 (hereafter <i>TSARL1</i>) was almost exclusively expressed in seeds, with significantly lower expression levels in sweet lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 8</a>). We identified the DNA motif bound by <i>M. truncatula</i> TSAR1 and TSAR2 within 2 kb upstream of the start codon in several saponin biosynthetic pathway genes in quinoa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>). Expression levels of these genes and several other genes in the saponin biosynthetic pathway were significantly downregulated in sweet lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 8</a>). Together, these results suggest that TSARL1 might be a functional TSAR orthologue, although whether this is due to shared ancestry or convergent evolution is unclear.</p><p>The <i>TSARL1</i> transcript was alternatively spliced in the sweet progeny of Kurmi and 0654. A SNP in the last position of exon 3 (G2078C) co-segregates with the presence of saponins in the Kurmi × 0654 progeny. The G2078C SNP alters the canonical intron/exon splice boundary (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>), probably leading to the alternative splicing at an upstream cryptic splice site in the sweet lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>). This alternative splicing of <i>TSARL1</i> results in a premature stop codon (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 9b</a>) and a truncated protein that modelling predicts to be compromised in its ability to form homodimers and/or to bind DNA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 9b–d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Information 8.8</a>), which are both necessary for regulation of transcription. All bitter strains in our re-sequencing pool share the same allele (G) found in the bitter progeny of Kurmi and 0654, whereas all sweet strains (Chucapaca, G205-95DK, Salcedo INIA, as well as the mapping population parents Kurmi and Atlas) but one (Pasankalla) contain the same allele (G2078C) as the sweet progeny. Notably, however, although the G2078C allele is present in the sequenced Atlas line, none of the sweet progeny in the Atlas × Carina Red population were found to have the G2078C allele. Additional sequencing of individual plants of the Atlas variety revealed a low level of heterogeneity within the variety for the <i>TSARL1</i> gene, with some plants containing the G2078C allele and others containing sequence insertions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Information 7.2.4</a>). Thus, it is likely that the Atlas plant used in the cross with Carina Red—which, importantly, was not the same plant used for sequencing—possessed a mutation other than the G2078C allele. Indeed, we found strong evidence of insertions in and around the <i>TSARL1</i> gene in all the sweet progeny of the Atlas × Carina Red population (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig14">Extended Data Fig. 10</a>). In particular, two exonic insertions in <i>TSARL1</i> in the sweet progeny probably inactivate the gene and result in a sweet phenotype. The identification of multiple, independent mutations in <i>TSARL1</i> that co-segregate with the sweet phenotype strongly suggests that this gene regulates the presence and absence of saponins in quinoa seeds. The early steps in the saponin biosynthetic pathway that are presumably regulated by TSARL1 are shared by other pathways, including sterol biosynthesis. Inactivation of these other pathways could be detrimental to normal plant growth, although we see no noticeable phenotypic differences between bitter and sweet siblings. It is possible that precursors needed for sterol biosynthesis are provided by the methylerythritol 4-phosphate (MEP) pathway in the plastid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Laule, O. et al. Crosstalk between cytosolic and plastidial pathways of isoprenoid biosynthesis in Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 100, 6866–6871 (2003)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2601">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Rodríguez-Concepción, M. &amp; Boronat, A. Breaking new ground in the regulation of the early steps of plant isoprenoid biosynthesis. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol . 25, 17–22 (2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2604">41</a></sup>. Future functional studies will help clarify these issues.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>As an emerging international crop, quinoa has great potential to enhance global food security. The high-quality reference genome assembly presented here will accelerate improvements of quinoa. Major breeding objectives for quinoa improvement include the development of shorter plants with fewer branches and more compact seed heads, increased heat and biotic stress tolerance, and the introgression of the sweet phenotype into commercial varieties. The identification of the likely causative mutation underlying the sweet phenotype not only provides insights into triterpenoid saponin biosynthesis, but also enables accelerated breeding of sweet commercial varieties using marker-assisted selection. The diversity present in the primary gene pool of quinoa, which we have begun to characterize, will also help direct future breeding strategies. The resources presented here also help to make quinoa a useful model for studying polyploid genome evolution and mechanisms of abiotic stress tolerance, in particular salinity tolerance.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The experiments were not randomized and the investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Quinoa sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We sequenced <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> Willd. (quinoa) accession PI 614886 (BioSample accession code SAMN04338310; also known as NSL 106399 and QQ74). DNA was extracted from leaf and flower tissue of a single plant, as described in the “Preparing <i>Arabidopsis</i> Genomic DNA for Size-Selected ~20 kb SMRTbell Libraries” protocol (<a href="http://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Shared-Protocol-Preparing-Arabidopsis-DNA-for-20-kb-SMRTbell-Libraries.pdf">http://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Shared-Protocol-Preparing-Arabidopsis-DNA-for-20-kb-SMRTbell-Libraries.pdf</a>). DNA was purified twice with Beckman Coulter Genomics AMPure XP magnetic beads and assessed by standard agarose gel electrophoresis and Thermo Fisher Scientific Qubit Fluorometry. 100 Single-Molecule Real-Time (SMRT) cells were run on the PacBio RS II system with the P6-C4 chemistry by DNALink (Seoul). <i>De novo</i> assembly was conducted using the smrtmake assembly pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/smrtmake">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/smrtmake</a>) and the Celera Assembler, and the draft assembly was polished using the quiver algorithm.</p><p>DNA was also sequenced using an Illumina HiSeq 2000 machine. For this, DNA was extracted from leaf tissue of a single soil-grown plant using the Qiagen DNeasy Plant Mini Kit. 500-bp paired-end (PE) libraries were prepared using the NEBNext Ultra DNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina. Sequencing reads were processed with Trimmomatic (v0.33)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2657">42</a></sup>, and reads &lt;75 nucleotides in length after trimming were removed from further analysis. The remaining high-quality reads were assembled with Velvet (v1.2.10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Zerbino, D. R. &amp; Birney, E. Velvet: algorithms for de novo short read assembly using de Bruijn graphs. Genome Res . 18, 821–829 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2661">43</a></sup> using a <i>k</i>-mer of 75.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Integrating BioNano optical maps with the PacBio assembly</h3><p>High-molecular-weight DNA was isolated and labelled from leaf tissue of three-week old quinoa plants according to standard BioNano protocols, using the single-stranded nicking endonuclease Nt.BspQI. Labelled DNA was imaged automatically using the BioNano Irys system and <i>de novo</i> assembled into consensus physical maps using the BioNano IrysView analysis software. The final <i>de novo</i> assembly used only single molecules with a minimum length of 150 kb and eight labels per molecule. PacBio-BioNano hybrid scaffolds were identified using IrysView’s hybrid scaffold alignment subprogram.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Chicago library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>Using the same DNA prepared for PacBio sequencing, a Chicago library was prepared as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res . 26, 342–350 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2690">10</a></sup>. The library was sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Scaffolding the PacBio and BioNano assemblies with HiRise</h3><p>Chicago sequence data (in FASTQ format) was used to scaffold the PacBio-BioNano hybrid assembly using HiRise, a software pipeline designed specifically for using Chicago data to assemble genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res . 26, 342–350 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2703">10</a></sup>. Chicago library sequences were aligned to the draft input assembly using a modified SNAP read mapper (<a href="http://snap.cs.berkeley.edu">http://snap.cs.berkeley.edu</a>). The separations of Chicago read pairs mapped within draft scaffolds were analysed by HiRise to produce a likelihood model, and the resulting likelihood model was used to identify putative mis-joins and score prospective joins.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Kurmi × 0654 population mapping and genetic marker analysis</h3><p>A population was developed by crossing Kurmi (green, sweet) and 0654 (red, bitter). Homozygous high- and low-saponin F<sub>2</sub> lines were identified by planting 12 F<sub>3</sub> seeds derived from each F<sub>2</sub> line, harvesting F<sub>4</sub> seed from these F<sub>3</sub> plants, and then performing foam tests on the F<sub>4</sub> seed. Phenotyping was validated using gas chromatography/mass spectrometry (GC/MS). RNA was extracted from inflorescences containing a mixture of flowers and seeds at various stages of development from the parents and 45 individual F<sub>3</sub> progeny. RNA extraction and Illumina sequencing were performed as described above. Sequencing reads from all lines were trimmed using Trimmomatic and mapped to the reference assembly using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2737">44</a></sup>, and SNPs were called using SAMtools mpileup (v1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2741">45</a></sup>.</p><p>For linkage mapping, markers were assigned to linkage groups on the basis of the grouping by JoinMap v4.1. Using the maximum likelihood algorithm of JoinMap, the order of the markers was determined; using this as start order and fixed order, regression mapping in JoinMap was used to determine the cM distances.</p><p>Genes differentially expressed between bitter and sweet lines and between green and red lines were identified using default parameters of the Cuffdiff function of the Cufflinks program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol . 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2751">46</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Atlas × Carina Red population mapping and genetic marker analysis</h3><p>A second mapping population was developed by crossing Atlas (sweet) and Carina Red (bitter). Bitter and sweet F<sub>2</sub> lines were identified by performing foam and taste tests on the F<sub>3</sub> seed. DNA sequencing was performed with DNA from the parents and 94 sweet F<sub>2</sub> lines, as described above, and sequencing reads were mapped to the reference assembly using BWA. SNPs were called in the parents and in a merged file containing all combined F<sub>2</sub> lines.</p><p>Genotype calls were generated for the 94 F<sub>2</sub> genotypes by summing up read counts over a sliding window of 500 variants, at all variant positions for which the parents were homozygous and polymorphic. Over each 500-variant stretch, all reads with Atlas alleles were summed, and all reads with the Carina Red allele were summed. Markers were assigned to linkage groups using JoinMap, with regression mapping used to obtain the genetic maps per linkage group.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Integrated linkage map</h3><p>The Kurmi × 0654 and Atlas × Carina Red maps were integrated with the previously published quinoa linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Maughan, P. J. et al. Single nucleotide polymorphism identification, characterization, and linkage mapping in quinoa. Plant Genome 5, 114–125 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2784">13</a></sup>, with the Kurmi × 0654 map being used as the reference for the positions of anchor markers and scaling. We selected markers from the same scaffold that were in the same 10,000-bp bin in the assembly.
