The anchor markers on the alternative map received the position of the Kurmi × 0654 map anchor marker in the integrated map. This process was repeated with anchor markers at the 100,000-bp bin level. The assumption is that at the 100,000-bp bin level recombination should essentially be zero. On this level, a regression of cM position on both maps yielded <i>R</i><sup>2</sup> values &gt;0.85 and often &gt;0.9, so the regression line can easily be used for interpolating the positions of the alternative map towards the corresponding position on the Kurmi × 0654 map. All Kurmi × 0654 markers went into the integrated map on their original position.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Chromosome pseudomolecules</h3><p>Pseudomolecules were assembled by concatenating scaffolds based on their order and orientation as determined from the integrated linkage map. An AGP (‘A Golden Path’) file was made that describes the positions of the scaffold-based assembly in coordinates of the pseudomolecule assembly, with 100 ‘N’s inserted between consecutive scaffolds. Based on these coordinates, custom scripts were used to generate the pseudomolecule assembly and to recoordinate the annotation file.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Sequencing and assembly of <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i></h3><p>DNA was extracted from <i>C. pallidicaule</i> (PI 478407) and <i>C. suecicum</i> (BYU 1480) and was sent to the Beijing Genomic Institute (BGI, Hong Kong) where one 180-bp PE library and two mate-pair libraries with insert sizes of 3 and 6 kb were prepared and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq platform to obtain 2 × 100-bp reads for each library. The generated reads were trimmed using the quality-based trimming tool Sickle (<a href="https://github.com/najoshi/sickle">https://github.com/najoshi/sickle</a>). The trimmed reads were then assembled using the ALLPATHS-LG assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2827">47</a></sup>, and GapCloser v1.12<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2831">48</a></sup> was used to resolve N spacers and gap lengths produced by the ALLPATHS-LG assembler.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome annotation</h3><p>Repeat families found in the genome assemblies of quinoa, <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i> (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Information 3</a>) were first independently identified <i>de novo</i> and classified using the software package RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Smit, A. F. A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2008–2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2856">49</a></sup>. RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013–2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2861">50</a></sup> was used to discover and identify repeats within the respective genomes.</p><p>AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2868">51</a></sup> was used for <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, using model training based on coding sequences from <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i>, <i>Beta vulgaris</i>, <i>Spinacia oleracea</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. RNA-seq and isoform sequencing reads generated from RNA of different tissues were mapped onto the reference genome using Bowtie 2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2887">52</a>) and GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2891">53</a></sup>, respectively. Hints with locations of potential intron–exon boundaries were generated from the alignment files with the software package BAM2hints in the MAKER package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res . 18, 188–196 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2895">54</a></sup>. MAKER with AUGUSTUS (intron–exon boundary hints provided from RNA-seq and isoform sequencing) was then used to predict genes in the repeat-masked reference genome. To help guide the prediction process, peptide sequences from <i>B. vulgaris</i> and the original quinoa full-length transcript (provided as EST evidence) were used by MAKER during the prediction. Genes were characterized for their putative function by performing a BLAST search of the peptide sequences against the UniProt database. PFAM domains and InterProScan ID were added to the gene models using the scripts provided in the MAKER package.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Re-sequencing</h3><p>The following quinoa accessions were chosen for DNA re-sequencing: 0654, Ollague, Real, Pasankalla (BYU 1202), Kurmi, CICA-17, Regalona (BYU 947), Salcedo INIA, G-205-95DK, Cherry Vanilla (BYU 1439), Chucapaca, Ku-2, PI 634921 (Ames 22157), Atlas and Carina Red. The following accessions of <i>C. berlandieri</i> were sequenced: var. <i>boscianum</i> (BYU 937), var. <i>macrocalycium</i> (BYU 803), var. <i>zschackei</i> (BYU 1314), var. <i>sinuatum</i> (BYU 14108), and subsp. <i>nuttaliae</i> (‘Huauzontle’). Two accessions of <i>C. hircinum</i> (BYU 566 and BYU 1101) were also sequenced. All sequencing was performed with an Illumina HiSeq 2000 machine, using either 125-bp (Atlas and Carina Red) or 100-bp (all other accessions) paired-end libraries. Reads were trimmed using Trimmomatic and mapped to the reference assembly using BWA (v0.7.10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2932">55</a></sup>. Read alignments were manipulated with SAMtools, and the mpileup function of SAMtools was used to call SNPs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Identification of orthologous genes</h3><p>Orthologous and paralogous gene clusters were identified using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res . 13, 2178–2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2944">28</a></sup>. Recommended settings were used for all-against-all BLASTP comparisons (Blast+ v2.3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2948">56</a></sup>) and OrthoMCL analyses. Custom Perl scripts were used to process OrthoMCL outputs for visualization with InteractiVenn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Heberle, H., Meirelles, G. V., da Silva, F. R., Telles, G. P. &amp; Minghim, R. InteractiVenn: a web-based tool for the analysis of sets through Venn diagrams. BMC Bioinformatics 16, 169 (2015)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2952">57</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Phylogenetic inference</h3><p>Using OrthoMCL, orthologous gene sets containing two copies in quinoa and one copy each in <i>C. pallidicaule</i>, <i>C. suecicum</i>, and <i>B. vulgaris</i> were identified. In total, 7,433 gene sets were chosen, and their amino acid sequences were aligned individually for each set using MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol . 30, 772–780 (2013)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2973">58</a></sup>. The 7,433 alignments were converted into PHYLIP format files by the seqret command in the EMBOSS package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet . 16, 276–277 (2000)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2977">59</a></sup>. Individual gene trees were then constructed using the maximum likelihood method using proml in PHYLIP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Felsenstein, J. PHYLIP (Phylogeny Inference Package) Version 3.6a3. &#xA;                    http://evolution.genetics.washington.edu/phylip.html&#xA;                    &#xA;                  " href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2982">60</a></sup>.</p><p>In addition, the genomic variants of all 25 sequenced taxa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 5</a>) relative to the reference sequence were called based on the mapped Illumina reads in 25 BAM files using SAMtools. To call variants in the reference genome (PI 614886), Illumina sequencing reads were mapped to the reference assembly. Variants were then filtered using VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2992">61</a></sup> and SAMtools, and the qualified SNPs were combined into a single VCF file which was used as an input into SNPhylo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Lee, T.-H., Guo, H., Wang, X., Kim, C. &amp; Paterson, A. H. SNPhylo: a pipeline to construct a phylogenetic tree from huge SNP data. BMC Genomics 15, 162 (2014)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d24281e2996">62</a></sup> to construct the phylogenetic relationship using maximum likelihood and 1,000 bootstrap iterations.</p><p>To identify <i>FT</i> homologues, the protein sequence from the <i>A. thaliana</i> flowering time gene <i>FT</i> was used as a BLAST query. Filtering for hits with an <i>E</i> value &lt;1 × e<sup>−3</sup> and with RNA-seq evidence resulted in the identification of four quinoa proteins. One quinoa protein (AUR62013052) appeared to be comprised of two tandem repeats which were separated for the purposes of phylogenetic analysis. For the construction of the phylogenetic tree, protein sequences from these five quinoa <i>FT</i> homologues were aligned using Clustal Omega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Sievers, F. et al. Fast, scalable generation of high-quality protein multiple sequence alignments using Clustal Omega. Mol. Syst. Biol . 7, 539 (2011)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3021">63</a></sup> along with two <i>B. vulgaris</i> (gene models: <i>Bv</i>FT1-miuf.t1, <i>Bv</i>FT2-eewx.t1) and one <i>A. thaliana</i> (AT1G65480.1) homologue. Phylogenetic analysis was performed with MEGA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol . 30, 2725–2729 (2013)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3038">64</a></sup> (v6.06). The JTT model was selected as the best fitting model. The initial phylogenetic tree was estimated using the neighbour joining method (bootstrap value = 50, Gaps/ Missing Data Treatment = Partial Deletion, Cutoff 95%), and the final tree was estimated using the maximum likelihood method with a bootstrap value of 1,000 replicates. The syntenic relationships between the coding sequences of the chromosomal regions surrounding these <i>FT</i> genes were visualized using the CoGE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J . 53, 661–673 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3045">65</a></sup> GEvo tool and the Multi-Genome Synteny Viewer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Revanna, K. V. et al. A web-based multi-genome synteny viewer for customized data. BMC Bioinformatics 13, 190 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3049">66</a></sup>.</p><p>The alignment of bHLH domains was performed with Clustal Omega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Sievers, F. et al. Fast, scalable generation of high-quality protein multiple sequence alignments using Clustal Omega. Mol. Syst. Biol . 7, 539 (2011)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3056">63</a></sup>, using sequences from Mertens <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Mertens, J. et al. The bHLH transcription factors TSAR1 and TSAR2 regulate triterpene saponin biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula. Plant Physiol . 170, 194–210 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3063">39</a></sup>. The phylogeny was inferred using the maximum likelihood method based on the JTT matrix-based model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Jones, D. T., Taylor, W. R. &amp; Thornton, J. M. The rapid generation of mutation data matrices from protein sequences. Comput. Appl. Biosci . 8, 275–282 (1992)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3067">67</a></sup>. Initial trees for the heuristic search were obtained automatically by applying Neighbour-Join and BioNJ algorithms to a matrix of pairwise distances estimated using a JTT model, and then selecting the topology with superior log likelihood value. All positions containing gaps and missing data were eliminated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Distinguishing and analysing the quinoa sub-genomes</h3><p>Trimmed PE Illumina sequencing reads that were used for the <i>de novo</i> assembly of <i>C. suecicum</i> and <i>C. pallidicaule</i> were mapped onto the reference quinoa genome using the default settings of BWA. For every base in the quinoa genome, the depth coverage of properly paired reads from the <i>C. suecicum</i> and <i>C. pallidicaule</i> mapping was calculated using the program GenomeCoverage in the BEDtools package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842 (2010)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3095">68</a></sup>. A custom Perl script was used to calculate the percentage of each scaffold with more than 5× coverage from both diploids. Scaffolds were assigned to the A or B sub-genome if &gt;65% of the bases were covered by reads from one diploid and &lt;25% of the bases were covered by reads from the other diploid. The relationship between the quinoa sub-genomes and the diploid species <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i> was presented in a circle proportional to their sizes using Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res . 19, 1639–1645 (2009)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3105">69</a></sup>. Orthologous regions in the three species were identified using BLASTN searches of the quinoa genome against each diploid genome individually. Single top BLASTN hits longer than 8 kb were selected and presented as links between the quinoa genome assembly (arranged in chromosomes, see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Information 7.3</a>) and the two diploid genome assemblies on the Circos plot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>).</p><p>Sub-genome synteny was analysed by plotting the positions of homoeologous pairs of A- and B-sub-genome pairs within the context of the 18 chromosomes using Circos. Synteny between the sub-genomes and <i>B. vulgaris</i> was assessed by first creating pseudomolecules by concatenating <i>s</i>caffolds which were known to be ordered and oriented within each of the nine chromosomes. Syntenic regions between these <i>B. vulgaris</i> chromosomes and those of quinoa were then identified using the recommended settings of the CoGe SynMap tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Lyons, E., Pedersen, B., Kane, J. &amp; Freeling, M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the rosids. Trop. Plant Biol . 1, 181–190 (2008)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3127">70</a></sup> and visualized using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res . 40, e49 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3131">71</a></sup> and VGSC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Xu, Y. et al. VGSC: a web-based vector graph toolkit of genome synteny and collinearity. BioMed Res. Int . 2016, 7823429 (2016)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3136">72</a></sup>. For the purposes of visualization, quinoa chromosomes <i>Cq</i>B05, <i>Cq</i>A08, <i>Cq</i>B11, <i>Cq</i>A15 and <i>Cq</i>B16 were inverted.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Saponin analyses</h3><p>Quinoa seeds were embedded in a 2% carboxymethylcellulose solution and frozen above liquid nitrogen. Sections of 50 μm thickness were obtained using a Reichert-Jung Frigocut 2800N, modified to use a Feather C35 blade holder and blades at −20 °C using a modified Kawamoto method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Kawamoto, T. Use of a new adhesive film for the preparation of multi-purpose fresh-frozen sections from hard tissues, whole-animals, insects and plants. Arch. Histol. Cytol . 66, 123–143 (2003)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3163">73</a></sup>. A 2,5-dihydroxybenzoic acid (Sigma-Aldrich) matrix (40 mg ml<sup>−1</sup> in 70% methanol) was applied using a HTX TM-Sprayer (HTX Technologies LLC) with attached LC20-AD HPLC pump (Shimadzu Scientific Instruments). Sections were vacuum dried in a desiccator before analysis. The optical image was generated using an Epson 4400 Flatbed Scanner at 4,800 d.p.i. For mass spectrometric analyses, a Bruker SolariX XR with 7T magnet was used. Images were generated using Bruker Compass FlexImaging 4.1. Data were normalized to the TIC, and brightness optimization was employed to enhance visualization of the distribution of selected compounds. Individual spectra were recalibrated using Bruker Compass DataAnalysis 4.4 to internally lock masses of known DHB clusters: C<sub>14</sub>H<sub>9</sub>O<sub>6</sub> = 273.039364 and C<sub>21</sub>H<sub>13</sub>O<sub>9</sub> = 409.055408 <i>m/z</i>. Accurate mass measurements for individual saponins and identified compounds were run using continuous accumulation of selected ions (CASI) using mass windows of 50–100 <i>m/z</i> and a transient of 4 megaword generating a transient of 2.93 s providing a mass resolving power of approximately 390,000 at 400 <i>m/z</i>. Lipids were putatively assigned by searching the LipidMaps database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Sud, M. et al. LMSD: LIPID MAPS structure database. Nucleic Acids Res . 35, D527–D532 (2007)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3192">74</a></sup> (<a href="http://www.lipidmaps.org">http://www.lipidmaps.org</a>) and lipid class confirmed by collision-induced dissociation using a 10 <i>m/z</i> window centred around the monoisotopic peak with collision energy of between 15–20 V.</p><p>Quinoa flowers were marked at anthesis, and seeds were sampled at 12, 16, 20 and 24 days after anthesis. A pool of five seeds from each time point was analysed using GC/MS.</p><p>Quantification of saponins was performed indirectly by quantifying oleanolic acid (OA) derived from the hydrolysis of saponins extracted from quinoa seeds. Derivatized solution was analysed using single quadrupole GC/MS system (Agilent 7890 GC/5975C MSD) equipped with EI source at ionisation energy of 70 eV. Chromatography separation was performed using DB-5MS fused silica capillary column (30m × 0.25 mm I.D., 0.25 μm film thickness; Agilent J&amp;W Scientific), chemically bonded with 5% phenyl 95% methylpolysiloxane cross-linked stationary phase. Helium was used as the carrier gas with constant flow rate of 1.0 ml min<sup>−1</sup>. The quantification of OA in each sample was performed using a standard curve based on standards of OA.</p><p>Specific, individual saponins were identified in quinoa using a preparation of 20 mg of seeds performed according a modified protocol from Giavalisco <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Giavalisco, P., Köhl, K., Hummel, J., Seiwert, B. &amp; Willmitzer, L. 13C isotope-labeled metabolomes allowing for improved compound annotation and relative quantification in liquid chromatography-mass spectrometry-based metabolomic research. Anal. Chem . 81, 6546–6551 (2009)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3220">75</a></sup>. Samples were measured with a Waters ACQUITY Reversed Phase Ultra Performance Liquid Chromatography (RP-UPLC) coupled to a Thermo-Fisher Exactive mass spectrometer, which consists of an electrospray ionisation source and an Orbitrap mass analyser. A C18 column was used for the hydrophilic measurements. Chromatograms were recorded in full-scan MS mode (mass range, 100 −1,500). Extraction of the LC/MS data was accomplished with the software REFINER MS 7.5 (GeneData).</p><p>SwissModel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Arnold, K., Bordoli, L., Kopp, J. &amp; Schwede, T. The SWISS-MODEL workspace: a web-based environment for protein structure homology modelling. Bioinformatics 22, 195–201 (2006)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3228">76</a></sup> was used to produce homology models for the bHLH region of AUR62017204, AUR62017206 and AUR62010677. RaptorX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Källberg, M., Margaryan, G., Wang, S., Ma, J. &amp; Xu, J. RaptorX server: a resource for template-based protein structure modeling. In Protein Structure Prediction (ed. Kihara, K. ) 17–27 (Springer, New York, 2014)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3232">77</a></sup> was used for prediction of secondary structure and disorder. QUARK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Xu, D. &amp; Zhang, Y. Ab initio protein structure assembly using continuous structure fragments and optimized knowledge-based force field. Proteins 80, 1715–1735 (2012)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3236">78</a></sup> was used for <i>ab initio</i> modelling of the C-terminal domain, and the DALI server<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Holm, L. &amp; Rosenström, P. Dali server: conservation mapping in 3D. Nucleic Acids Res . 38, W545–W549 (2010)" href="/articles/nature21370#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d24281e3243">79</a></sup> was used for 3D homology searches of this region. Models were manually inspected and evaluated using the PyMOL program (<a href="http://pymol.org">http://pymol.org</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Data availability statement</h3><p>The genome assemblies and sequence data for <i>C. quinoa</i>, <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i> were deposited at NCBI under BioProject codes <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA306026">PRJNA306026</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA326220">PRJNA326220</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA326219">PRJNA326219</a>, respectively. Additional accessions numbers for deposited data can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#MOESM119">Supplementary Data 9</a>. The quinoa genome can also be accessed at <a href="http://www.cbrc.kaust.edu.sa/chenopodiumdb/">http://www.cbrc.kaust.edu.sa/chenopodiumdb/</a> and on the Phytozome database (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net/">http://www.phytozome.net/</a>).</p></div></div></section>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Mark+Tester" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Mark+Tester%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>M.T. and D.E.J. conceived the project. M.T. supervised the research. M.T., D.E.J., Y.S.H., D.J.L., S.M.S., P.J.M., E.N.J., E.N.v.L., C.G.v.d.L. and T.G. conceived and designed the experiments and managed particular components of the project. Y.S.H. led the bioinformatics analyses. Y.S.H., D.J.L., D.E.J. and B.L. did most of the compilation of the genome scaffolds and the genomic analyses. Y.S.H. annotated the genome and undertook the analysis of repetitive elements. E.N.v.L. did the final genetic mapping. E.N.J. and P.J.M. provided germplasm, oversaw sequencing of the diploid genomes, oversaw the BioNano mapping, led the comparative genomics work and shared their knowledge of quinoa. S.M.S. oversaw all saponin-related analyses. C.T.M., A.R.S., T.B., E.S., K.M., E.N.J. and P.J.M. prepared materials and undertook sequencing activities. K.M., H.O. and S.N. oversaw all phylogenetic analyses. Y.H.W. and G.G. analysed the microRNAs. N.S., N.M.K., R.R.R., X.G. and S.A.-B. did saponin analyses. N.D. and C.J. analysed the genes related to flowering time. S.T.A. and M.A.M. did the computational structure–function analysis. B.A.B. and U.R. did the metabolomics imaging. All authors contributed to the writing of the paper. D.E.J., Y.S.H., D.J.L., S.M.S., B.L. and M.T. organised the manuscript. D.E.J. and M.T. coordinated the project. D.E.J., Y.S.H., D.J.L., S.M.S. and B.L. contributed equally.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature21370/email/correspondent/c1/new">Mark Tester</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>

Stein for their contribution to the peer review of this work.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec25" data-title="Extended data figures and tables"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec25-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec25">Extended data figures and tables</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec25-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig5"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/5" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig5_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 1 Quinoa linkage map.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Linkage map generated by integrating maps from three independent quinoa populations. Black bands in each linkage group represent mapped markers. Letters next to each linkage group name indicate putative assignments of the linkage group to the A or B sub-genome. Numbers of each linkage group correspond to the numbering of chromosomes (for example, LG01(B) is equivalent to <i>Cq</i>B01). Scale bar, cM.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 2 Quinoa genome annotation.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Representative gene model showing mapped RNA sequencing reads generated using Illumina or isoform sequencing technologies. The top and middle panels show isoform sequencing and RNA-seq reads, respectively, that have been mapped to the chromosomal location containing the AUR62017258 gene model, which is shown on the bottom panel. Light grey lines in the top two panels indicate regions where reads were split to indicate introns positions. Full-length isoform sequencing reads were able to span the 5′ untranslated region, all exons, and the 3′ untranslated region in a single read. <b>b</b>, Gene density and GC% in 100-kb windows in quinoa chromosomes. <b>c</b>, The frequency of annotation edit distance (AED) scores for the assemblies of quinoa (blue), <i>C. pallidicaule</i> (red) and <i>C. suecicum</i> (green).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 3 Distribution of SNPs in the quinoa genome.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Frequency of SNPs in the sequenced quinoa accessions, relative to the reference quinoa genome assembly, in a 1-Mb window size. <i>y</i> axis scale is from 0 to 10,000 SNPs. The innermost track shows scaffolds arranged according to their placement in the linkage groups, with scaffolds coloured according to sub-genome assignment based on mapping sequencing reads from <i>C. pallidicaule</i> and <i>C. suecicum</i>, as in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature21370#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>. From inside to outside, the remaining tracks show SNPs in PI 634921, Atlas, CICA-17, Carina Red, Cherry Vanilla, Chucapaca, G-205-95DK, Ku-2, Kurmi, 0654, Ollague, Pasankalla, Real, Regalona and Salcedo INIA.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 4 Orthologous genes in quinoa, <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i>, <i>Beta vulgaris</i> and <i>Spinacia oleracea</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Venn diagram representing the number of protein-coding gene clusters shared between, or distinct to, the indicated species. The number in parentheses indicates the total number of genes contained within the associated clusters.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 5 Syntenic relationships supporting the orthologous relationships between quinoa and <i>Beta vulgaris FT</i> genes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Chromosomal regions containing Bv<i>FT1</i> and the quinoa genes AUR62013052 (Cq<i>FT1B</i>) and AUR62010060 (Cq<i>FT1A</i>). The annotation for AUR62013052 appears to contain two duplicate genes, which we have separated and designated as Cq<i>FT1B-1</i> and Cq<i>FT1B-2</i>. <b>b</b>, Chromosomal regions containing Bv<i>FT2</i> and the quinoa genes AUR62000271 (Cq<i>FT2A</i>) and AUR62006619 (Cq<i>FT2B</i>). Green and grey arrows represent <i>FT1</i> and <i>FT2</i> genes, respectively, and coloured lines connect orthologous coding sequences surrounding the <i>FT</i> genes.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 6 Imaging mass spectrometry visualization of saponins in the pericarp of a quinoa seed.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Table lists saponin annotation. <sup>a</sup>PA, phytolaccagenic acid; Hed, hederagenin; SA, serjanic acid; OA, oleanolic acid; AG489, AG515, AG487 refer to new aglycones with a specific <i>m/z.</i> Pen, pentose; Hex, hexose; HexA, corresponding sugar acid. <sup>b</sup>Pairs of saponins with the same chemical formula but different retention times. Image label indicates saponin ID. Data for sweet lines, including parent 0654, were consolidated into one box plot (Sweet). Box plots show median values (solid horizontal lines), 25th and 75th percentile values (box), 90th percentile values (whiskers) and outlier values (open circles). Quantification was performed using standards of oleanolic acid. Letters above each box plot represent statistically significant (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) differences between groups based on Games Howell post hoc test.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 8 Variation in the thickness of seed coat layers in bitter and sweet lines.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Representative scanning electron microscopy image of a quinoa seed cross-section, showing an example of a region (white box) from which measurements were taken. Scale bar, 30 μm <b>c</b>, Thickness of the internal seed coat layer in bitter and sweet lines. <b>d</b>, Thickness of the external seed coat layer in bitter and sweet lines. Letters above each box plot represent statistically significant (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001) differences between groups based on ANOVA. Box plots show median values (solid horizontal lines), 25th and 75th percentile values (box), 90th percentile values (whiskers) and outlier values (open circles). <i>n</i> = 91, 160, 54, and 129 for internal bitter, internal sweet, external bitter, and external sweet, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 9 Prediction of TSARL1 function using phylogenetics and computational modelling.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Maximum likelihood tree of select bHLH peptide sequences from quinoa, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, showing the close evolutionary relationship between the quinoa bHLH TSARL1 (AUR62017204) and the <i>M. truncatula</i> bHLHs TSAR1 and TSAR2 (MEDTR7G080780 and MEDTR4G066460, respectively). Branch values represent the percentage of 500 bootstrap replicates that support the topology. Subclades of the bHLH family, as defined in <i>A. thaliana</i>, are indicated on the right. <b>b</b>, Sequence alignment of TSARL bHLH sequences. Underlined blue, bHLH homology domain (solid line, helix; dashed line, loop). Underlined red, C-terminal domain. Boxed, residues that confer specificity to E box DNA binding. Green boxed Arg, residue that selects for the central CG dinucleotide. AUR62017204_AS designates the alternatively spliced protein. <b>c</b>, Schematic drawing of full-length TSARL1 (AUR62017204). Dashed lines indicate regions predicted to be flexible. Boxed region shows C-terminal domain lost in alternative splice variant. <b>d</b>, Zoomed-in view of the black dashed box in <b>c</b>, showing TSARL1 specifically binding the CACGHG motif.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig14"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature21370/figures/14" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature21370/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature21370_Fig14_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 10 Structural variation in <i>TSARL1</i> in the sweet progeny of Atlas and Carina Red.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Screenshot from Integrative Genomics Viewer (IGV) showing read alignment results in a 3-kb region (track 1, top) around <i>TSARL1</i> (track 5, bottom). Shown are alignments for Atlas (track 2), Carina Red (track 3), and the merged F<sub>2</sub> sweet lines (track 4). In tracks 2–4, the top portion shows coverage, and the bottom portion shows individual reads. The F<sub>2</sub> merged data (track 4) shows evidence of three structural variants (labelled A, B and C) relative to the reference, whereas Atlas (track 2) only shows evidence of the G2078C SNP (labelled D). <b>b</b>, <b>c</b>, Screenshots from IGV showing before (<b>a</b>) and after (<b>b</b>) local re-assembly of the reference sequence to include the B insertion site shown in panel <b>a</b>, illustrating the effect on read mapping from the merged F<sub>2</sub> sweet lines.                    id="title-link-2">

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">The quinoa genome sequence</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>In this issue, Mark Tester and colleagues report a reference genome for quinoa (<i>Chenopodium quinoa</i>), a highly nutritious crop that can grow under a wide range of environmental conditions. Long-read sequencing combined with optical, chromosome-contact and genetic maps was used to generate the allotetraploid genome. The authors also sequenced the genomes of additional diploid and tetraploid <i>Chenopodium</i> species, characterizing genetic diversity and the evolution of sub-genomes in the crop. In the process, Tester and colleagues identified a transcription factor that regulates the biosynthesis of bitter-tasting saponins in quinoa, as well as markers that might be used to develop sweet commercial varieties.</p></div></section></div></aside>
